study was to determine the structure, governance, \nand experience to date of established critical care organizations (COO) in \nNorth American academic medical centers. A CCO had to have an \nadvanced governance structure, that is, headed by a physician with \nprimary governance over the majority, if not all, of the ICUs and critical \ncare operations in the medical center.\n• They identified 37 CCOs. 10 were subsequently excluded leaving 27 COOs \nidentified. \n• The first CCO has been in place for more than 30 years, four CCOs were set \nup between 1990 and 2000, five between 2001 and 2005, eight between \n2006 and 2010, and six between 2011 and 2014. Only one CCO was \nexclusively a pediatric CCO.\n• Approximately 38% (9/24) identified their CCO officially as a department, \n21% (5/24) a center, 13% (3/24) a system, 13% (3/24) an operations \ncommittee, 4%(1/24) an institute, 4% (1/24) a service line, 4% (1/24) a \nsignature program, and 4% (1/24) a critical care hospital.\nAttachment 2 – Slide 19 of 21 19\n• The objective of the study was to determine the structure, governance, \nand experience to date of established critical care organizations (COO) in \nNorth American academic medical centers. A CCO had to have an \nadvanced governance structure, that is, headed by a physician with \nprimary governance over the majority, if not all, of the ICUs and critical \ncare operations in the medical center.\n• They identified 37 CCOs. 10 were subsequently excluded leaving 27 COOs \nidentified. \n• The first CCO has been in place for more than 30 years, four CCOs were set \nup between 1990 and 2000, five between 2001 and 2005, eight between \n2006 and 2010, and six between 2011 and 2014. Only one CCO was \nexclusively a pediatric CCO.\n• Approximately 38% (9/24) identified their CCO officially as a department, \n21% (5/24) a center, 13% (3/24) a system, 13% (3/24) an operations \ncommittee, 4%(1/24) an institute, 4% (1/24) a service line, 4% (1/24) a \nsignature program, and 4% (1/24) a critical care hospital.\nThey Missed Us!\nAttachment 2 – Slide 20 of 21 20\nQuestions?\nAttachment 2 – Slide 21 of 21 21\nProposal for the establishment of an Academic Department of Critical Care Medicine in the Faculty of \nMedicine and Dentistry \nOverarching Goal \n• To strengthen the academic mission of the FoMD by further development of the academic\ncritical care medicine. \nDevelopment as a Specialty  \n• The first multisystem critical care units in Canada were developed in the late 1960’s in Toronto,\nEdmonton and Winnipeg. \n• A fellowship program in Critical Care Medicine developed in Edmonton by Dr. E.G. King in 1970,\nwas one of the first of its kind. \n• In the mid 1980s, Dr. King and Dr. Tom Noseworthy were instrumental in the development of\nthe Critical Care Medicine training programs of the Royal College of Physicians and Surgeons of \nCanada (RCPSC).   \n• In 1989, the University of Alberta Critical Care Residency Program was one of the first to be\naccredited by the RCPSC. \n• In 2000, the Regional Program Clinical Director for Critical Care, Dr. Noel Gibney, was appointed\nas Divisional Director.  This allowed the academic Division and the clinical Department to merge \ntheir vision, mission and goals.  Faculty members could now hold a primary appointment in the \nDCCM and secondary appointments, if desired, in other departments. \n• In 2012, Alberta Health Services created Strategic Clinical Networks (SCNs). Aligned with the\nDepartmental structure of the AHS, critical care was one of the initial specialties to be \nrecognized with network foundation.  \nClinical \n• Critical Care units are becoming an increasing component of hospitalized care. A 2010 study\ndemonstrated the need for intensive care continues to increase. Over 5 years in the US there\nhas been a 4% decrease in the total number of hospital beds but an increase in ICU beds by 7%.\nHospital non-ICU inpatient days increased by 5% while ICU inpatient days increased by 10%.\nImportantly, annual critical care medicine costs increased by 44%. (Crit Care Med 2010, 38:65-\n71). It is expected the ratio of ICU beds to hospital beds will continue to rise.\n• The Division of Critical Care Medicine, University of Alberta/Department of Critical Care\nMedicine, Edmonton Zone, Alberta Health Sciences (DCCM) is (one of) the largest integrated\nacademic and clinical critical care units in Canada.\n• DCCM provides Intensivist coverage for 8 Critical Care Units (multisystem and specialized) in 5\nhospitals totaling 121 beds. The DCCM provides care for over 6500 patients per year (over\n37500 patient days). Thousands of hospitalized patients are seen by our Medical Emergency or\nRapid Response teams annually.\n• The DCCM has 50 members, of whom 36 have their primary academic appointment in the\nDCCM.  The DCCM is larger, in terms of members and Faculty, than the two current Canadian\nacademic Departments of Critical Care Medicine (Calgary and Dalhousie).\n• The DCCM has 9 primarily appointed Faculty members (5 GFT and 4 special continuing\nappointments). In addition, the Division has six GFT members secondarily appointed to Critical\nCare. With these 15 members, the DCCM is larger than 7 Departments in the FoMD based on\ndata provided in 2014.\nAttachment 3 - Page 1 of 2 1\nAttachment 4\n• The DCCM was successful in recruiting 2 additional special continuing status Faculty this year\nand will join the FoMD in 2015 and 2016. Finally, the DCCM has a translational anesthesiology\nbased Intensivist researcher in the Clinical Investigator Program and will join Faculty in 2018.\nThis will mean the proposed Academic Department will have 16 GFT/SCS members by 2018.\nResearch \n• The academic productivity of the proposed Department has increased steadily. In 2013, 79\nunique publications were documented by Divisional members. This increased to 94 in 2014. It is \nexpected 2015 will see over 100 unique publications \n• Since 2009, research activity in the DCCM has been successful in securing funding for the\nfollowing (mostly peer-reviewed) grant applications: Canadian Institutes for Health Research, \nAlberta Innovates: Health Solutions Alberta Heritage Fund for Medical Research), Canadian \nIntensive Care Foundation, Royal Alexandra Hospital Foundation, University of Alberta Hospital \nFoundation, Rick Hansen Foundation Man in Motion, Physician Services Incorporated, Children’s \nHospital of Eastern Ontario Research Institute, Royal Alexandra Hospital Nursing Research Fund, \nTransplant Fund Value Added, Canadian Blood Services, Edmonton Civic Employees Charitable \nAssistance Fund, Women and Children’s Health Research Institute Innovation Grant, Centre for \nExcellence for Gastrointestinal Inflammation and Immunity Research, and Royal Alexandra \nHospital Nursing Research Fund. Annual grant funding has increased from $443,129 in 2013 to \n$1,454,231 in 2015. \n• Dr. Sean Bagshaw, a clinician scientist and the Director of Research for the DCCM, holds a Tier II\nCanada Research Chair in Critical Care Nephrology. \nEducation \n• The DCCM operates an accredited 2 year RCPSC Adult Critical Care Medicine training program.\n• Currently training six residents that have already completed primary specialty\n• The program has recently been approved for growth of an additional resident per year.\n• The DCCM trains one or two international critical care residents a year in addition to the RCPSC\nresidency program trainees\n• The DCCM has developed a research fellowship for graduate training and a cardiac intensive\ncare fellowship (one and two year)\n• In addition, the training program is also responsible for providing Critical Care exposure of two\nto three months duration to approximately 125 residents from the Department of Surgery,\nMedicine and its subspecialties, Emergency Medicine, Anesthesia, Neurosciences,\nCardiovascular Surgery, Obstetrics and Gynecology as required by the RCPSC.\n• Graduate student supervision in health services research, clinical epidemiology, translational\nresearch is increasing.\nOther Significant Contributions to Society \n• Canadian Critical Care Society – End of Life Care, Goals of Care Designations\n• Canadian Critical Care Trials Group\n• DCCM is integral to Trauma Services, Burn Treatment, Cardiac Surgery, Neurosciences and\nDonation and Transplantation with U of A Hospital a referral centre for Western Canada\nAttachment 3 - Our site has a very active role in the education of residents from various \nspecialties and in training our own RCPSC Adult Critical Care Medicine residents, but we feel we have \nbeen underperforming with respect to academic productivity. \nOver the past year, we have successfully recruited an Otolaryngologist/Intensivist who is completing his \nMaster’s degree in Health Quality and an Aneasthesiologist/Intensivist who is completing her PhD in \nPhysiology in the Clinical Investigator Program. FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of May 30, 2016 \nOUTLINE OF ISSUE \nAgenda Title: Academic Governance and Delegated Authority \nMotions:  \nWhereas at its meeting of 25 January 2016 the General Faculties Council (GFC) approved the \nfollowing motions: \nTHAT the General Faculties Council will review the delegation of its authorities to all of \nits current committees, and by 30 June 2016 vote on whether to renew or revise these \ndelegated authorities. \nand \nTHAT the review of the governance and operations of the General Faculties Council is \nto be informed by the June 2013 report \"A Reflection on the State of Academic \nGovernance at the University of Alberta.\" \nAt its meeting of 30 May 2016, the General Faculties Council: \na. approves the interim renewal of delegated authorities to all GFC standing committees until \nrevised “Terms of Reference” modifying the delegated authorities of these committees are \npresented to it for its final approval on or before 30 April 2017; \nb. affirms its authority to make recommendations to the Board of Governors of the University \nof Alberta on any matter that it deems to be of interest to the University; and \nc. affirms its ultimate overarching authority to recall to it at any time any matter being \nconsidered by its standing committees under delegated authority; \nd. approves the creation of an ad hoc committee to lead, during the 2016-17 academic year, \nthe process for the revision of the “Terms of Reference” for GFC and all of its standing \ncommittees, with “Terms of Reference” for and composition of this ad hoc committee to be \napproved by GFC at its September 2016 meeting; \ne. directs the ad hoc committee to bring forward to GFC its recommendations for revisions to \nthe “Terms of Reference” for GFC and its Standing Committees addressing delegated \nauthority for final approval on or before 30 April 2017; \nf. directs that during the 2016-17 academic year GFC meet as often as necessary between \nSeptember and April to discuss and consider proposals of the ad hoc committee for the \nrevision of the “Terms of Reference” of the General Faculties Council and its standing \ncommittees;  and \ng. moves that until such time as the “Terms of Reference” for the General Faculties Council \nand its standing committees are revised, anything in the current “Terms of Reference” for \nthe General Faculties Council or any of its standing committees not consistent with any of \nthe above is null and void. \nItem No. 8 \nGENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of May 30, Post-Secondary Learning Act (PSLA) \n“Powers of general faculties council \n26(1)Subject to the authority of the board, a general faculties council is \nresponsible for the academic affairs of the university and, without restricting \nthe generality of the foregoing, has the authority to  \n (a) exercise any power of a faculty council that the general faculties council \nconsiders desirable to exercise;  \n (b) consider and make decisions on the reports of the faculty councils as to \nthe programs of study in the faculties;  \n (c) determine all programs of study to which clause (b) does not apply that \nare to be offered by the university for credit toward the requirements for any \ndegree or diploma;  \nItem No. 8 \nGENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of May 30, 2016 \n (d) determine the timetables for examinations and for lectures and other \ninstruction in each faculty;  \n (e) consider and make decisions on the reports of faculty councils as to the \nappointment of examiners and the conduct and results of examinations in \nthe faculties;  \n (f) provide for the granting and conferring of degrees other than honorary \ndegrees;  \n (g) provide for the preparation and publication of the university calendar;  \n (h) hear and determine appeals from the decisions of faculty  \ncouncils on applications, requests or petitions by students and others;  \n (i) consider all matters reported to it by any faculty council and \ncommunicate its opinion or action on those matters to the faculty council \nconcerned;  \n (j) determine the date for the beginning and end of lectures in the university \nand also the beginning and end of each university term; \n(k) make rules and regulations for the management and operation of \nlibraries;  \n (l) recommend to the board the establishment of faculties, schools, \ndepartments, chairs and programs of study in the university in any subject \nthat the general faculties council thinks fit;  \n (m) make rules and regulations respecting academic awards;  \n (n) determine standards and policies respecting the admission of persons \nto the university as students;  \n (o) make recommendations to the board with respect to affiliation with \nother institutions, academic planning, campus planning, a building program, \nthe budget, the regulation of residences and dining halls, procedures in \nrespect of appointments, promotions, salaries, tenure and dismissals, and \nany other matters considered by the general faculties council to be of \ninterest to the university;  \n (p) authorize lecturing and teaching on the university premises by persons \nother than members of the staff of the university;  \n (q) authorize a school to have a school council of the same nature and with \nthe same powers, duties and functions as a faculty council and, in its \ndiscretion, revoke any authority so given.  \n(2) Any recommendations from the general faculties council to the board \nmust be transmitted to the board through the president. \n(3) A general faculties council may delegate any of its powers, duties and \nfunctions under this Act, including the powers referred to in section 31, as it \nsees fit and may prescribe conditions governing the exercise or \nperformance of any delegated power, duty or function, including the power \nof subdelegation.” \n2. General Faculties Council Terms of Reference / 3. Mandate \n“Powers Retained by General Faculties Council \nAll powers and responsibilities under Section 26 of the PSLA not \nexpressly delegated now or in the future shall be retained by General \nFaculties Council. (GFC 02 DEC 1966) \nThe issues which remain with GFC or which would be referred by a \nStanding Committee to GFC would generally be in the nature of the \nfollowing: \n• high level strategic and stewardship policy issues or matters of \nsignificant risk to the University; \n• alterations to the mandate, terms of reference, composition, or \nstructure of a Standing Committee; \nItem No. 8 \nGENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of May 30, 2016 \n• those things which a Standing Committee considers to be of major \nstrategic significance to or long-term impact on the University; \n• those matters on which, in the opinion of a Standing Committee chair, \nthere has been a strong division of opinion within the Standing \nCommittee; and \n• issues in which there is a lack of clarity as to which Standing \nCommittee is responsible. \n3. General Faculties Council Terms of Reference / 4. General \nFaculties Council Procedures \n …. \na) New Committees and Ad Hoc Committees  \nWhen GFC establishes a new committee, GFC (or delegate \nbody) shall provide clear, explicit, and detailed terms of \nreference. (GFC 24 NOV 1980)  \nb)  Ad Hoc Committees: When an ad hoc committee is in doubt as \nto the scope of its terms of reference, it shall seek clarification \nand guidance from the Executive Committee of General Faculties \nCouncil. (EXEC JUN 1971)  \nWhen GFC establishes an ad hoc committee, a reporting date \nshall be set by the Secretary to GFC. (EXEC 03 MAY 1982)  \nAd hoc committees shall be considered as being discharged \nonce their reports are received or accepted by General Faculties \nCouncil unless the committee recommends otherwise, or General \nFaculties Council requests it to continue. (GFC 29 APR 1968) \n4. GFC Executive Committee Terms of Reference/3. Mandate of \nthe Committee:  “To act as the executive body of General Faculties \nCouncil and, in general, carry out the functions delegated to it by \nGeneral Faculties Council.”  \n[…] \n“5. Agendas of General Faculties Council \nGFC has delegated to the Executive Committee the authority to \ndecide which items are placed on a GFC Agenda, and the order in \nwhich those agenda items appear on each GFC agenda.  \nWhen ordering items, the GFC Executive Committee will be mindful of \nany matters that are of particular concern to students during March \nand April so that the student leaders who bring those items forward \nare able to address these items at GFC before their terms end. (EXEC \n06 NOV 2006)  \n[…] \nWith respect to recommendations from other bodies and other GFC \ncommittees, however, the role of the Executive Committee shall be to \nexamine and debate the substance of reports or recommendations \nand to decide if an item is ready to be forwarded to the full governing \nbody. The Executive Committee may decide to refer a proposal back \nItem No. 8 \nGENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of May 30, 2016 \nto the originating body, to refer the proposal to another body or \nindividual for study or review, or to take other action in order to ready \na proposal for consideration by General Faculties Council. When the \nGFC Executive Committee forwards a proposal to GFC, it shall make \na recommendation that GFC endorse; endorse with suggested \namendments; not endorse; or forward the proposal with no comment.” \nRouting (Include meeting dates) \nParticipation: \n(parties who have seen the \nproposal and in what capacity) \nGFC Executive Committee (for discussion) – December 7, 2015 and \nJanuary 11, 2016 \nGeneral Faculties Council (for discussion) – January 25, 2016 \nGFC Executive Committee (for discussion) – February 1 and March 7, \n2016 \nGFC Executive Committee Working Group  on Academic Governance \n(for discussion)– February/March  \nGeneral Faculties Council (for discussion) – March 21, 2016 \nAcademic Governance Working Group (for discussion) – April 2016 \nGFC Executive Committee (for discussion) – April 11, 2016 \nGeneral Faculties Council – May 2, 2016 \nAcademic Governance Working Group (for discussion) – May  2016 \nGeneral Faculties Council Executive – May 16, 2016 \nApproval Route (Governance) \n(including meeting dates) \nGFC Executive Committee – May 16, 2016 \nGeneral Faculties Council – May 30, 2016 \nFinal Approver General Faculties Council – May 30, 2016 \nAttachments: \nAttachment 1 (pages 1-6): Excerpt from the Post-Secondary Learning Act, sections 26 and 31. \nhttp://www.qp.alberta.ca/documents/Acts/p19p5.pdf\nmailto:marion.haggarty-france@ualberta.ca\nmailto:meg.brolley@ualberta.ca\n2003\nSection 23  Chapter P-19.5 POST-SECONDARY LEARNING ACT \n21\nGeneral Faculties Council \nComposition of general faculties council \n23   Each university must have a general faculties council \nconsisting of \n(a) the following persons who are members by virtue of their \noffices: \n(i) the president, who is the chair; \n(ii) the vice-presidents;\n(iii) the dean of each faculty; \n(iv) the director of each school; \n(v) the chief librarian; \n(vi) the director of extension, or if none, the officer \nperforming comparable functions; \n(vii) the registrar;\n(b) the members elected under section 24 to represent the \nfaculties and the schools that have school councils; \n(c) the following student members: \n(i) 2 students nominated by the council of the students \nassociation; \n(ii) if there is a graduate students association, one student \nnominated by the council of the association; \n(d) the members appointed from the staff and students of the \nuniversity under section 25. \nElected members \n24(1)  The faculty council of each faculty and the school council of \neach school that has a school council may elect from the full-time \nmembers of the academic staff of the faculty or school the number \nof members to the general faculties council that may be assigned to \nit pursuant to subsection (2). \nAttachment 1\nGFC - May 2, 2016\n2003\nSection 25  Chapter P-19.5 POST-SECONDARY LEARNING ACT \n22\n(2)  On the direction of the general faculties council from time to \ntime the registrar \n(a) shall establish the total number of elected members to be on \nthe general faculties council, which shall be twice the \nnumber of persons who are members of the general faculties \ncouncil by virtue of their offices, and \n(b) shall determine and assign to each faculty and school the \nnumber of members that may be elected by that faculty or \nschool, which so far as is reasonably possible shall be in the \nsame proportion to the total number of elected members as \nthe number of full-time members of the academic staff of \nthe faculty or school is to the total number of full-time \nmembers of the academic staff of all the faculties and \nschools. \n(3)  A member elected under subsection (1) holds office for a term \nof 3 years or until the member’s successor is elected. \n(4)  Notwithstanding subsection (3), a member elected under \nsubsection (1) ceases to hold office if the member ceases to be a \nfull-time member of the academic staff of the faculty or school. \n(5)  Notwithstanding subsection (3), a faculty council or school \ncouncil \n(a) shall, as circumstances require, elect members to hold office \nfor one-year or 2-year terms so as to provide overlapping \nterms of office for the representatives of the faculty or \nschool, and \n(b) may, when a member ceases to hold office before the expiry \nof that member’s term of office, elect a new member to hold \noffice for the remainder of the unexpired term. \nAppointed members \n25  The members of the general faculties council referred to in \nsection 23(a), (b) and (c) shall appoint the appointed members of \nthe general faculties council from the staff and students of the \nuniversity, in the number and for the terms of office that the elected \nand student members and persons who are members by virtue of \ntheir offices determine.  \nPowers of general faculties council \n26(1)  Subject to the authority of the board, a general faculties \ncouncil is responsible for the academic affairs of the university and, \n2003\nSection 26  Chapter P-19.5 POST-SECONDARY LEARNING ACT \n23\nwithout restricting the generality of the foregoing, has the authority \nto \n(a) exercise any power of a faculty council that the general \nfaculties council considers desirable to exercise; \n(b) consider and make decisions on the reports of the faculty \ncouncils as to the programs of study in the faculties; \n(c) determine all programs of study to which clause (b) does not \napply that are to be offered by the university for credit \ntoward the requirements for any degree or diploma; \n(d) determine the timetables for examinations and for lectures \nand other instruction in each faculty; \n(e) consider and make decisions on the reports of faculty \ncouncils as to the appointment of examiners and the conduct \nand results of examinations in the faculties; \n(f) provide for the granting and conferring of degrees other than \nhonorary degrees; \n(g) provide for the preparation and publication of the university \ncalendar; \n(h) hear and determine appeals from the decisions of faculty \ncouncils on applications, requests or petitions by students \nand others; \n(i) consider all matters reported to it by any faculty council and \ncommunicate its opinion or action on those matters to the \nfaculty council concerned; \n(j) determine the date for the beginning and end of lectures in \nthe university and also the beginning and end of each \nuniversity term; \n(k) make rules and regulations for the management and \noperation of libraries; \n(l) recommend to the board the establishment of faculties, \nschools, departments, chairs and programs of study in the \nuniversity in any subject that the general faculties council \nthinks fit; \n(m) make rules and regulations respecting academic awards; \n(n) determine standards and policies respecting the admission of \npersons to the university as students; \n2003\nSection 27  Chapter P-19.5 POST-SECONDARY LEARNING ACT \n24\n(o) make recommendations to the board with respect to \naffiliation with other institutions, academic planning, \ncampus planning, a building program, the budget, the \nregulation of residences and dining halls, procedures in \nrespect of appointments, promotions, salaries, tenure and \ndismissals, and any other matters considered by the general \nfaculties council to be of interest to the university; \n(p) authorize lecturing and teaching on the university premises \nby persons other than members of the staff of the university; \n(q) authorize a school to have a school council of the same \nnature and with the same powers, duties and functions as a \nfaculty council and, in its discretion, revoke any authority so \ngiven. \n(2)  Any recommendations from the general faculties council to the \nboard must be transmitted to the board through the president. \n(3)  A general faculties council may delegate any of its powers, \nduties and functions under this Act, including the powers referred \nto in section 31, as it sees fit and may prescribe conditions \ngoverning the exercise or performance of any delegated power, \nduty or function, including the power of subdelegation. \n2003 cP-19.5 s26;2009 c11 s2 \n2003\nSection 30  Chapter P-19.5 POST-SECONDARY LEARNING ACT \n26\nStudent Affairs \nStudent discipline \n31(1)  The general faculties council has general supervision of \nstudent affairs at a university and in particular, but without \nrestricting the generality of the foregoing, the general faculties \ncouncil may \n(a) subject to a right of appeal to the board, discipline students \nattending the university, and the power to discipline \nincludes the power \n(i) to fine students, \n(ii) to suspend the right of students to attend the university \nor to participate in any student activities, or both, and \n(iii) to expel students from the university; \n(b) delegate its power to discipline students in any particular \ncase or generally to any person or body of persons, subject \nto any conditions with respect to the exercise of any \ndelegated power that it considers proper; \n2003\nSection 32  Chapter P-19.5 POST-SECONDARY LEARNING ACT \n27\n(c) give to a student organization of the university the powers to \ngovern the conduct of students it represents that the general \nfaculties council considers proper. \n(2)  Any powers to govern the conduct of students given to a \nstudent organization pursuant to subsection (1)(c) are subject to the \noverriding control of the board, the president and the general \nfaculties council. \nA\ncadem\nic G\novernance W\norking G\nroup \nR\neport to M\nay 30, 2016 G\nFC\nD\nuncan E\nlliott, M\nirko van der B\naan, \nC\narolyn This \nitem was discussed by the GFC Executive Committee who agreed that this is an important issue but that it \nwould benefit from a wider review.  As such, the GFC Executive Committee recommends a modification \nwhich extends the original motion to include not only the review of how USRIs might be modified, \naugmented, or possibly replaced, but also considers the development and implementation of a more robust \nsolution to teaching assessment and evaluation. \nMotion:  THAT the General Faculties Council, on the recommendation of the GFC Executive Committee, \nrequest that the GFC Committee on the Learning Environment report by 30 April 2017, on research into the \nuse of student rating mechanisms of instruction in university courses. This will be informed by a critical \nreview of the University of Alberta’s existing Universal Student Ratings of Instruction (USRIs) and their use \nfor assessment and evaluation of teaching as well as a broad review of possible methods of multifaceted \nassessment and evaluation of teaching. The ultimate objective will be to satisfy the Institutional Strategic \nPlan: For the Public Good strategy to: Provide robust supports, tools, and training to develop and assess \nteaching quality, using qualitative and quantitative criteria that are fair, equitable, and meaningful across \ndisciplines. \nItem   \nAction Requested Approval Recommendation  Discussion/Advice Information \nProposed by Steven Dew, Provost and Vice-President (Academic) \nPresenter Steven Dew, Provost and Vice-President (Academic) \nSubject Teaching and Learning and Teaching Evaluation and the use of the \nUniversal Student Ratings of Instruction (USRI) as an evaluative tool \nDetails \nResponsibility Provost and Vice-President (Academic) \nThe Purpose of the Proposal is \n(please be specific) \nThe Provost's Office supports the idea of examining the USRIs, but \nwould like to undertake this task with a broader consideration of how \nUSRIs might be modified, augmented, or possibly replaced over time.  \nSince the GFC Policy 111 specifies required use of the USRIs as a \nmeans of achieving instructor feedback from students, the USRIs cannot \nbe simply removed without a replacement. Additionally, as specified in \nthe GFC Policy 111, there must be multifaceted assessment and \nevaluation of teaching and while this exists in some areas, it is not being \ncarried out throughout the university.  Thus a broader approach to this \nproblem would support the development and implementation of a more \nrobust solution to teaching assessment and evaluation.  \nThe Impact of the Proposal is An examination of both the USRI and the need for multifaceted review of \nteaching will lead to rescission of the current GFC Policy 111 and \ncreation of a new UAPPOL policy to support teaching and learning and \nteaching assessment and evaluation. Recommendations on the \nrescission of the GFC Policy 111 and creation of new UAPPOL policy to \nsupport Teaching, Learning and Teaching Evaluation will come forward \nItem No. 9 \nGENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of May 30, 2016 \nto GFC for approval.  \nReplaces/Revises (eg, policies, \nresolutions) \nN/A \nTimeline/Implementation Date April 30, 2017 \nEstimated Cost N/A \nSources of Funding N/A \nNotes Both GFC policy and collective agreements mandate that there must be \na student evaluation of teaching. GFC policy 111 Teaching and Learning and Teaching Evaluation \n“111.2 Teaching Evaluation  \n1. Evaluation of teaching at the University of Alberta serves two purposes: \na. Summative – Evaluation provides a review and overview of an instructor’s \nteaching that is an essential element in promotion and tenure decisions. In its \nsummative form, teaching evaluation forms a basis for rewarding excellence, as \nwell as the basis for withholding reward. \nb. Formative – Evaluation provides helpful feedback to teachers by identifying \nteaching strengths and weaknesses and, in so doing, giving guidance for the \nimprovement or refinement of teaching skills. \n2. Evaluation of teaching must be multifaceted. Multifaceted evaluation shall \ninclude the Universal Student Ratings of Instruction set out in Section 111.3 and \nother methods of assessing teaching designed within individual Faculties to \nrespond to the particular conditions of that Faculty. Such assessments shall \ninclude one or more of the following: input from administrators, peers, self, \nundergraduate and graduate students, and alumni. \n3. Recognizing that the evaluation of teaching at the University shall be \nmultifaceted, Faculty Evaluation Committee (FEC) decisions concerning tenure, \npromotion or unsatisfactory teaching performance must be based on more than \none indicator of the adequacy of teaching. \n4. Assessment of teaching involving input from administrators, peers, self, \nalumni, or undergraduate and graduate students in addition to the Universal \nStudent Ratings of Instruction should occur annually prior to tenure. For \ncontinuing faculty (ie, Categories A1.1, A1.5 and A1.6), such assessment will \noccur at least triennially.  \n5. The University shall continue to support University Teaching Services in its \neducation programming which is focused on the development and improvement \nof teaching and learning and its efforts to enhance research in university \nteaching. \n111.3 Universal Student Ratings of Instruction \nIn recognition of the University's commitment to teaching, the General Faculties \nItem No. 9 \nGENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of May 30, 2016 \nCouncil endorses a system of Universal Student Ratings of Instruction. This \nsystem, however, is only one part of the multi-faceted approach described in \nSection 111.2. \nThe Universal Student Ratings of Instruction are administered electronically via a \nsystem known as the eUSRI system.   \nThe Universal Student Ratings of Instruction are designed to provide a minimal \nuniversity-wide base of information on student ratings to the parties listed in this \nSection. With this purpose in mind, the General Faculties Council adopts the \nfollowing policies: \nA. All Faculties will ensure that evaluation of all instructors and courses will take \nplace each time a course is offered. The term ‘instructors’ is meant to include \ntenured professors, tenure-track professors, sessional instructors, clinical \ninstructors, field supervisors and graduate teaching assistants with \nresponsibilities for courses. \n[…] \nD. The anonymity of student responses to the Universal Student Ratings of \nInstruction is of fundamental importance in maintaining student confidentiality \nand encouraging the free expression of views. Under normal circumstances, the \nanonymity of students will be protected. Universal Student Ratings of Instruction \noffer an avenue of feedback, including feedback critical of instructors. \n[…] \nG. The numerical summaries for the ten Universal Student Ratings of Instruction \nquestions will be reported to the instructor, the Chair, Director or Dean and \nstudents. \n[…] \nI. All results given out to students, Chairs, Directors and Deans will have the \nfollowing cautionary preface: \nStudent questionnaires form an important part of evaluating teaching \neffectiveness but cannot be taken alone as a complete assessment of an \ninstructor or course. Factors other than an instructor’s teaching ability may \ninfluence ratings. These factors include class size, class level, Faculty, time in \nclass, required versus optional course, grade expectations, student GPA, \ngender, race, ethnicity, age of both students and instructors. \n[…] \nJ. Nothing in this section will prevent instructors from seeking other means of \nfeedback from students during the term.” \nThe full GFC Policy 111 Teaching and Learning and Teaching Evaluation is \navailable at: \nhttp://www.gfcpolicymanual.ualberta.ca/111TeachingandLearningandTeach.aspx \n5. During the intervening years there \nhave been a great many changes to the academy; and during the last few years, the last year or so especially, \nthere have been many reports on research into such instruments that suggest that they tend to function in \ninequitable and even discriminatory ways. I would therefore very much like to see the General Faculties \nCouncil's Committee on the Learning Environment review this research and report on it to GFC, and to provide \nat that time its recommendations for any changes to the USRIs that would be appropriate or indeed necessary \nif the General Faculties Council is to continue to endorse their use.  \nTiming of Request \nI put this forward motion now rather than in the Fall for two reasons: so that the expertise of any member of the \nCLE whose term may be ending at June 30th may be captured on this issue to assist with the committee's work \nnext year; and to assist the University in getting out ahead of a policy document that is scheduled to come \nforward to the Council of the Canadian Association of University Teachers (CAUT) in the Fall. I would like the \nGeneral Faculties Council to be positioned to take an informed decision next year on what about our current \nUSRIs would need to change if the GFC were to continue to endorse them. \nIt is my understanding that there was some work done by the Committee on the Learning Environment on this \nfront six years ago in 2010, but it is time for the committee to do further work on this issue in light of the \nresearch on these instruments that has emerged during the last couple of years. Only with that work can the \nGeneral Faculties Council proceed to informed decision-making on this important matter. \nYours sincerely, \nCarolyn \n--  \nCarolyn Sale \nAssociate Professor, Department of English & Film Studies \nVice-President, Association of Academic Staff University of Alberta (AASUA) \n3-77 Humanities Centre \nEdmonton, Alberta \nCanada T6G 2E5 \nPhone:   Apologies: none due to budget cuts to the Faculty of Arts in 2009-2010. \nFax:       780.492.8142 \nBlog:      artssquared.wordpress.com \nmailto:sale@ualberta.ca\nmailto:dturpin@ualberta.ca\ntel:780.492.8142\nhttp://artssquared.wordpress.com/\nItem No. 10 \nGENERAL FACULTIES COUNCIL \nFor the Meeting of May 30, 2016 \nOUTLINE OF ISSUE \nAgenda Title: Proposal for a new Scheduling Initiative for Augustana Faculty  \nMotion:  N/A  \nItem   \nAction Requested Approval Recommendation  Discussion/Advice Information \nProposed by Dr. Allen Berger, Dean, Augustana Faculty \nPresenter Dr. Allen Berger, Dean, Augustana Faculty; Dr. Karsten Mündel, \nAssociate Dean, Academic, Augustana Faculty \nSubject New Scheduling Initiative for Augustana Faculty \nDetails \nResponsibility Provost and Vice-President (Academic) \nThe Purpose of the Proposal is \n(please be specific) \nAs a residential, undergraduate, liberal arts and science faculty within \nthe University of Alberta, the Augustana Campus places a special value \non community-based education, international and outdoor educational \nexperiences, undergraduate research and connections to rural and \nAboriginal communities. Faculty Consultation........................................................6 \nIV. FAQ.........................................................................................7 \n 2 \nI.  SHORT INFORMATON SUMMARY REGARDING '3-11' \nWhat Is It? \nOn May 4, 2015, the Augustana Faculty Council, after two years of study and with an overall \nmajority of 89%, passed the following motion: \n“THAT the Augustana Faculty implement, effective 2017-18, a new academic schedule with \nthe Fall and Winter semesters each consisting of a 3-week term followed by an 11-week \nterm and adopt a new timetable for the 11-week terms as outlined in Schedule A, and \ninvestigate the feasibility of an additional 3-week block term in the spring.” \nVision \nAs a residential, undergraduate, liberal arts and science faculty within the University of \nAlberta, the Augustana Campus places a special value on community-based education, \ninternational and outdoor educational experiences, undergraduate research and \nconnections to rural and aboriginal communities. In this context, the 3-11 framework \nserves a dual-purpose vision: 1) to provide a rhythm for each academic year that advances \nexperiential learning opportunities for students, connections between these opportunities \nand more traditional classroom work, and pedagogical innovation by faculty; 2) to create a \nclearer niche for Augustana and further differentiate the undergraduate student experience \nat Augustana from alternatives on North Campus or at other Alberta universities. \nBackground \nSeveral undergraduate universities (e.g., Quest University in Canada; Colorado College and \nCornell College in the U.S.) have organized the entire undergraduate experience on a block \ncalendar in which students take one course at a time and faculty only teach one course at a \ntime. Other universities have traditionally placed courses into 10-11 week quarters instead \nof 13-15 week semesters. Based on personal experiences that Augustana faculty members \nhave had with both systems and the visit of a delegation to Quest University, we have \ndecided on a hybrid approach that we believe offers significant advantages and flexibility. \nAdditional Benefits \nThe new academic calendar and timetable also offer opportunities to address: student \nworkload issues (particularly important for student mental health challenges that are \nincreasingly recognized as critical); faculty workload issues (particularly the need to \naccommodate research expectations in a predominantly teaching Faculty); the need for \nmore flexibility in degree completion times; the possibility of developing a first-year \nseminar, understood across North America to be a “high-impact practice;” the need to \nprovide opportunities for first and second-year students to ‘sample’ courses (i.e., to \ncommit less time to an interest that may or may not result in a change of major); the \nchallenge of how to accommodate students’ varying learning styles; and Augustana’s \nrelated goals of enrolment and revenue growth (the latter not only determined by the \nnumber of degree-seeking students but also by the number of community members who \nmay be interested in taking, and paying for, 3-week courses).  \n 3 \nOrganization of Implementation \nAugustana recognizes that implementation of these changes will require the support of \npartners within the central University of Alberta administration, including key personnel in \nthe Registrar’s Office and the Office of the Provost. Preliminary conversations with \nRegistrar Lisa Collins have helped identify the likely players. In addition, Augustana has \ncreated a 3-11 Task Force that includes representatives from all academic departments, \nstudent services and the Augustana Students’ Association. Leadership will be provided by \nVice Dean Anne-Marie Link and Associate Dean Academic Karsten Mündel. Other faculty \nand staff will be brought in as necessary to help work out specific details related to \nimplementation. Significant work will also be done within our three Department Councils. \nTimeline \nThe motion passed by the Augustana Faculty Council envisions two years for preparation, \nwith roll-out for the 2017/18 Academic Year. Most of the logistical, policy, and procedural \nwork of implementation must be completed by July 2016 for calendar and recruitment \nmaterials to accurately reflect the new Augustana realities. An important first task of \nimplementation, therefore, is collectively to work backwards from that date to establish the \ndeadlines for the various subtasks outlined below. \nKey Tasks \nThe work of implementation at this point falls into three overlapping categories. The \nfollowing list is presented as a starting point for our work with Central partners, \nrecognizing that other tasks will be added as a result of initial planning discussions.  \nNew Timetable \nAdoption of the 3-11 academic schedule requires us to adopt a new timetable for both the \n3-week and 11-week course periods. The timetable for 3-week courses will be relatively \neasy to implement, as traditional courses will be scheduled for approximately 3 hours per \nday with morning, afternoon and evening timeslots. The 11-week schedule ensures that \nthere will be the same number of instructional minutes in an 11-week term as in the \ncurrent 13-week term. \nNew Academic Schedule \n● setting up Campus Solutions to deal with the course schedule structure at \nAugustana \n● changes to relevant academic procedures and regulations in relation to the schedule \nand timetable changes (the group will need to create early on a full list of these \nprocedures and regulations) \nAugustana Tasks \nWe recognize that a series of Augustana-specific tasks need to take place in advance of the \n3-11 start date. These include: work on relevant promotional and recruitment materials; \ninvestments in faculty development; curriculum revisions; development of a First Year \nExperience seminar course for the first three-week course period as a pilot project.  \n 4 \nII.  BACKGROUND \nA Learning Experience Research Committee (LERC) was convened after the Augustana \nFaculty Council meeting of October 2013 to research and consider alternative learning \nexperience opportunities that might be implemented at Augustana Campus and to produce \na specific proposal for an alternative learning experience that reflects the values and \nculture of learning at Augustana. The point of producing a specific proposal was to facilitate \na substantive discussion in the Faculty.  Following this substantive discussion and \nconsultation [SEE BELOW], the proposal, which was to become known as '3-11' was \nsubsequently passed with an 89% majority at the Augustana Faculty Council meeting in \nMay 2015.   \nRationale for Proposal  \nA. The proposal was guided by the values and culture of learning articulated in The \nAugustana Faculty Academic Performance Measures (December 2012) and by the evaluation \nand recommendations of the Academic Unit Review of the Augustana Faculty (2011) since  \n(1) both reports have been accepted by the Augustana Faculty and University of Alberta  \n(2) the self-description of Augustana in The Augustana Faculty Academic Performance \nMeasures was a supplement to the Academic Measures for Budgeting Process 2013-14 \ndistributed by the Office of the Provost and as such is used to determine budget allocations \nto Augustana  \nB. This proposal aimed to strengthen the three characteristics that are unique to Augustana \n(according to The Augustana Faculty Academic Performance Measures):  \n(1) International Experiences  \n(2) Community-Based Learning  \n(3) Rural Connectedness  \nIn all three areas, the intention is to increase both the number of participating students and \nthe number of courses in each area.  \nC. The proposal also aimed to implement specific recommendations from the Academic Unit \nReview:  \n(1) to “push ahead confidently in making programming even more distinctive than is \nalready the case” by “strengthening and extending the core curriculum”;  \n(2) expand opportunities for “undergraduate research and creative work”;  \n(3) focus on “the liberal education provided to students regardless of major,” particularly \nwith regards to international experiences, community-based learning, and rural \nconnectedness.  \nD. The proposed learning experiences are high-impact practices that have all been tried \nand successfully implemented at one or more COPLAC institutions. This evidence suggests \nthat these practices could also work at Augustana if tailored to our specific situation. \nMoreover, the specific learning experiences in the proposal are constructed to meet current \nstudent needs (such as greater flexibility). \nThe \"3-11\" calendar was the major component of the resulting proposal, along with a First \nYear Experience. \n 5 \nThe objective of 3-11 is to provide students and faculty with more flexibility as well as to \nfacilitate unique experiential learning opportunities. Augustana could institute a 3 week course \nperiod at the beginning of the Fall and/or Winter term followed by an 11 week course period.  \nThese 3 and 11 week courses are accommodated within the existing Fall and Winter terms \nof the University of Alberta and do not change the number of contact hours, number of \ncourses taken by students over each term (normally 5) or number of courses taught by \nfaculty (normally 3).  \n 6 \nIII.  FACULTY CONSULTATION RE:  3-11 CALENDAR \nThe change to the 3-11 Calendar system formed the major part of the LERC proposal.   \nThe LERC committee consisted of representatives from all three academic departments and \nfrom the Augustana Students Association.  In the 2014-15 academic year it organized \ndiscussions regarding 3-11 at departmental meetings, at Faculty Council meetings and a series \nof formal consultations with faculty and staff.  The formal consultation dates are listed here: \n6 January          noon - 1:30         \n13 January       noon - 1:30          \n20 January       noon - 1:30 \n27 January       noon - 1:30  \n2 February      12:30 – 2:00              \n2 March            12:30 – 2:00              \n10 March          noon - 1:30          \n24 March          noon - 1:30            \n13 April            noon - 1:30  \nThere were between ten and twenty participants at the consultations outside Faculty \nCouncil meetings and departmental meetings. \nThe Faculty Council met in May 2015 to review, discuss and consider the material brought forth \nby the LERC committee and the results of its consultations.  The following motion was \nsubsequently put before the Faculty Council: \nTHAT the Augustana Faculty implement, effective 2017-18, a new academic \nschedule with the Fall and Winter semesters each consisting of a 3-\nweek term followed by an 11-week term and adopt a new timetable for \nthe 11-week terms as outlined in Schedule A, and investigate the \nfeasibility of an additional 3-week block term in the spring.  \nThe procedure for voting was that of a 'Super Majority', a decision-making procedure that had \nbeen voted on and carried at the December 1, 2014 meeting of the Faculty Council. This \nrequired that the motion for implementation of 3-11 needed to attain both a simple majority in \neach academic department followed by a two-thirds majority of YES votes at Faculty Council in \nwhich all members were to vote (faculty members, APOs, student representatives). \nThe majorities were achieved in all departments and an 89% YES vote was achieved at the \nFaculty Council meeting of May 2015. \nDuring that meeting, an Implementation Committee was constituted, which consists of \nrepresentatives from all academic departments, from the ASA, and from Residence \nLife/Student Services. \nConsultation continues as a major focus of the Implementation Committee. \n 7 \nIV.  FAQ \nStudent FAQ \n1) Why is Augustana doing this? \nAfter more than 2 years of consultation with students, faculty and staff, about \nways to improve the Augustana Advantage, we decided to implement a new \nacademic calendar and a First Year Experience. The new calendar will introduce \ngreater flexibility and foster the development of new experiential, travel and \nlearning opportunities. The First Year Experience will give students a seminar \nexperience in their first year to introduce them to learning in a Liberal Arts \ncontext. \n2) How does the new Augustana Academic Calendar work? \nThe Fall Term will consist of a 3-week course period in which students would take \n1 course followed by an 11-week course period in which students would take 4 \ncourses. The Winter Term would also have a 3-week course period and an \n11-week course period. There will be an increasing effort to also offer courses in a \nSpring 3-week course period. \n3) What is the implementation timeline? \nThe new academic calendar and First Year Experience will come into effect for the \n2017-18 academic year. The whole Augustana Campus will adopt the new \ncalendar. Students starting in the 2017-18 academic year will take the First Year \nSeminar class. \n4) Will I still be able to graduate in 4 years? \nAbsolutely! Students will still be able to take 5 courses in the Fall and Winter \nTerms and therefore be able to graduate in 4 years. As we increase our course \nofferings in the Spring and Summer Terms, students will also be able to pick up \nadditional courses to introduce greater flexibility into their schedules. \n5) What is the Augustana First Year Experience? \nThe Augustana First Year Experience is specifically designed to help students to \nmake the transition to university life and learning in a Liberal Arts context. There \nare two key components: a) the new student orientation and b) the Augustana \nFirst Year Seminar (FYS). The FYS will explore a specific topic in a small \nseminar-style setting. Students will be exposed to small-group discussion and will \nexplore a topic from a variety of academic disciplines and viewpoints. The new \nstudent orientation will work with the non-academic aspects of the transition to \nuniversity life. \n 8 \n6) What do I need to do to maintain full­time status in the new Augustana \nAcademic Calendar? \nFull-time status will continue to be a minimum of 9 credits per Term. Students have the \nflexibility to choose where in the Term to take their courses (for example, they could take \n3 credits in the 3-week course period and 6 credits in the 11-week course period). As is \ncurrently the case, specific scholarships and sponsorships may require a greater number \nof credits to maintain eligibility. In any case, the number of credits will be calculated at the \nTerm level (and not the block level). \n7) What kinds of 3­week courses will be offered in the fall and winter? \nThere will be a variety of courses offered in all 3-week course periods. These 3-week \ncourses will take advantage of the different time frame and the fact that students and \nprofessors are not involved in any other courses at the same time. \n8) Can I take September or January off? \nOne of the advantages of the new Academic Calendar is the choice it provides for \nscheduling. So, yes, you may be able to build an academic schedule that does not have any \nclasses in September or January for some of the Terms of your academic career at \nAugustana. However, depending on your major and other components of your academic \nprogram, it may be more challenging to complete your degree in 4 years if you take a \nbreak in September or January. \n9) Can I transfer my courses from Augustana to other universities? \nNone of the changes we are making to the academic schedule will impact the \ntransferability of these courses to other institutions. As at present, not all courses will \nalways transfer to a given program in a given receiving institution. Students are always \nadvised to check with a receiving institution about transferability. \n10) Are there any impacts on costs? \nNo. The costs for courses will continue to be calculated in the same manner as they are \nnow. Augustana will continue to follow the same fee assessment schedule as it currently \ndoes. Residence fees will continue to be assessed on a Term basis. \n11) Will my transcript look the same as it does now? \nYes. The transcripts will continue to look the same as they do now, listing courses by term \ntaken. \nDo you have other questions? Please contact Sarah Ross in the Dean’s Office  \nross3@ualberta.ca and she will find you an answer (and include it here in the FAQ). \nmailto:ross3@ualberta.ca\n 9 \nStaff FAQ \n1. How does the new Augustana Academic Calendar work? \nThe Fall Term will consist of a 3-week course period in which students would take 1 \ncourse and an 11-week course period in which students would take 4 courses. The Winter \nTerm would also have a 3-week course period and 11-week course period. There will be \nan increasing effort to offer courses in a Spring 3-week course period. \n2. What is the implementation timeline? \nThe new academic calendar are First Year Experience will come into effect for the \n2017-18 academic year. The whole Augustana Campus will adopt the new calendar. \nStudents starting in the 2017-18 academic year will take the First Year Seminar class. \n3. How do I raise issues related to the impact of the new Academic Calendar on \nmy work?  \nThe first person to ask is your direct manager.  Additionally, Anne-Marie and Karsten are \nscheduling meetings with every unit on campus during the Fall Term. Those meetings will \nbe a further chance to ask questions about the impact of these changes on your work \nrhythms. \n4. How does full-time status work in the new Augustana Academic Calendar? \nFull-time status will continue to be 9 credits per Term. Students have the flexibility to \nchoose where in the Term to take their courses (for example, they could take one course in \nthe 3-week course period and 2 courses in the 11-week course period). As is currently the \ncase, specific scholarships and sponsorships may require a greater number of credits to \nmaintain eligibility. In any case, the number of credits will be calculated at the Term level \n(and not the period of weeks in which the course occurs). \nDo you have other questions? Units of course weight form a part \nof the degree requirements and are also used to \ncalculate a student’s Grade Point Average (GPA). \n(2)  Junior-level Courses: Courses numbered 100-199. \n(3)  Senior-level Courses: Courses numbered 200-599. \n(4)  Term: The standard University of Alberta \ninstructional periods are from September to December \n(Fall Term), January to April (Winter Term), May/June \n(Spring Term), and July/August (Summer Term).  \nAugustana Faculty further divides these periods as \nfollows: \n(5)  Single-term Course: A term course is a course with \na course weight of 3 that normally extends over one \nUniversity term. Certain courses are offered over a \nsingle term with weights of *1, *1.5, and *2. \n(6)  Two-term Course: A two-term course is a course \nwith a course weight of 6 that normally extends over \ntwo consecutive University terms. Certain courses are \noffered over Fall/Winter with weights of *2, *3, and \n*4. \n(7)  Year Status: Each student who has been admitted \nto a degree program at Augustana is classified \naccording to year status as follows: \na. *0-23 earned: first-year standing. First-year \nstudents are normally not eligible to take courses \nnumbered 200 or higher. \nb. *24-53 earned: second-year standing. \nc. *54-83 earned: third-year standing. \nd. *84 or more earned: fourth-year standing. \n(8)  Course load:  \na. Those who choose not to attend \nmust assume whatever risks are involved. Students \nshould pay close attention to any further requirements \non attendance and class participation indicated on \ncourse outlines (see §23.4). \nDare to Discover Values:  \n• Create a dynamic, discovery-based learning environment by \naiming for a mix of undergraduate and graduate students typical \nof leading public research universities.  \n• Enhance the global perspective and intercultural climate at the \nuniversity by celebrating and drawing upon the diversity within it.  \n• Build strong partnerships with the capital region, the cities of \nEdmonton and Camrose, other urban and rural Alberta \ncommunities, and all orders of government to fulfil our \nresponsibility as Alberta's university, a leader on the national \nstage with global connections.  \n• Enhance relationships with other nations to create learning \nopportunities for students and research collaborations to address \nglobal challenges and initiatives that foster mutual understanding, \nglobal peace, and prosperity.  \n• Engage with, serve, and draw strength from the diversity of our \nexternal communities, in particular Aboriginal, Franco-Albertan, \nmulticultural, rural, and northern communities.  \n• Promote administrative effectiveness and good governance by \nimproving communication among units, enhancing collaboration, \nimplementing transformative ideas, and revising organizational \nstructures. \nCompliance with Legislation, \nPolicy and/or Procedure \nRelevant to the Proposal \n1. Post-Secondary Learning Act (PSLA): The PSLA gives GFC \nresponsibility, subject to the authority of the Board of Governors, over \nacademic affairs. Overall, this report confirms what our everyday interactions with graduate students \nsuggest: that our programs are attractive (application rates have traditionally been high), our \nstudents are strong (consistently showing high admission GPAs), our support systems are \nworking (the attrition rate rarely exceeds 20%), and our supervision is conscientious (our time to \ncompletion is stable, and competitive with Canadian and American averages).   \nGraduate recruitment, admission and enrolment are highly decentralized. Our 7500 graduate \nstudents work in over 300 research areas with 250 official specializations. The Faculty of \nGraduate Studies and Research exerts a centripetal force in this context, and is in a prime \nposition to collate disparate data and connect local to national and international trends. By its \nnature, this report looks to the past: five to ten years, depending on the availability and reliability \nof data. The historical knowledge it offers will allow us to chart the right course for the future. \nAmong the important insights contained here: after a decade of growth, doctoral enrolments \nmay be softening, particularly in Arts and Science. Since PhD enrolment is closely tied to the \nsize of the professoriate and to available research dollars, both of which have stalled, this \nmakes sense. (Anecdotal evidence suggests this is a national trend.) On the other hand, \ndemand for course-based Master’s programs continues to surge. The Province of Alberta does \nnot have a specific designation for Professional Master’s programs, but a deeper dive into the \nenrolment data presented here might support the call for such a distinction. The information on \ntime to completion demonstrates that international students complete their degrees more quickly \nthan do domestic students (including Permanent Residents). Attrition rates are highest in thesis-\nbased programs. The cost – to students and to the institution – of doctoral non-completion is \nhigh, and deserves further thought. The number of First Nations, Métis and Inuit (FNMI) \napplicants and students is very low. If the University identifies FNMI recruitment as a strategic \ngoal, special consideration must be given to bringing those students into graduate programs. \nA few notes on data and methodology: all graduate enrolment data is expressed in terms of \nheadcount rather than Full Load Equivalent (FLE). Headcount is the only reliable way to count \ngraduate students, since the FLE calculation over-estimates the number of students, due to a \nchanging denominator (number of credits). Wherever possible, we have used the December 1st \nheadcount, which provides the most accurate snapshot, capturing all of the students who start \ntheir studies in September and excluding everyone who completes their program during the fall \nsemester. However, even the December 1st headcount is only a snapshot: in a minority of \nFaculties, there can be a significant influx of students during the other semesters, and that will \nnot be captured here. \nWherever possible, we have used the University of Alberta data warehouse as our statistical \nsource. For some of the more historical analysis, however, we have used in-house FGSR \nfigures. These figures do not always square with one another. In future editions, we will be able \nto rely on the data warehouse exclusively, solving the problem of inconsistency.  \nYour comments and questions – about this report or its successors – are very welcome. One \nelement that we know would be welcome is benchmarking to national and international \nstandards. Frustratingly, much of the comparative data is privacy-protected or out of date; we \nhope that future enrolment reports will provide more comparative data.  \n3 \nI must close with a note of appreciation for my predecessor, Dr. Mazi Shirvani, who compiled a \ngreat deal of data concerning graduate students in his seven years as Dean of the Faculty. I \nalso appreciate the contributions of Deborah Williams, Gurpinder Gandhara and Denise Giles. \nOf course, I accept responsibility for any shortcomings. \nFGSR welcomes the opportunity to be a key partner in strategic enrolment management and \npresents this report with great pride. \nHeather Zwicker, PhD \nInterim Vice-Provost and Dean, Faculty of Graduate Studies and Research \n3 February 2016 \n4 \n1. Enrolment \nIn this section, all numbers are the standard December 1st headcounts, as reported to Statistics \nCanada and the provincial government.  \nVariation in graduate enrolment from one academic year to the next is due to three independent \nfactors. The number increases by the total number of new registrations, and it decreases by \nthe number of those leaving, through convocation or through attrition (see section 3). As an \naggregate measure, enrolment variations have to be understood with reference to the changes \nin these three factors. \nIt is also worth noting that this enrolment figure is a point-in-time snapshot, and does not show \nthe total number of graduate students who have been on campus at various points during the \nyear. (December 1st headcounts are a snapshot of the Fall Term registrations only.)  \n1.1 Graduate Enrolment by Degree Type \nThis graph demonstrates the overall increase in graduate enrolment over the last decade: from \n6541 (2511 doctoral +2111 Master’s thesis-based +1919 Master’s course-based) to 7228 (2777 \ndoctoral + 1966 Master’s thesis-based +2408 Master’s course-based). As the following table \nshows, FNMI students are particularly interested in course-based Master’s programs. \nEnrolment \nSource: FGSR Statistics \n0\n20\n40\n60\n80\n100\n120\n140\nHe\nad\nco\nun\nt \nAcademic Year \nApplications Applicants Admitted Applicants Registered\n0\n10\n20\n30\n40\n50\n60\n70\nHe\nad\nco\nun\nt \nAcademic Year \nDoctoral Master's Thesis-Based Master's Course-Based\n13 \n2. Applications and Admissions \nFollowing a fairly rapid rise for several years, the number of applicants for admission3  has been \ndeclining since the 2012-2013 academic year, in both the domestic and the international \napplicant pools. Expanding the pool of qualified applicants is a component of active recruitment \nstrategies. \nThe yield rate (the percentage of registrations resulting from offers of admission) has been \nextremely steady, at about two thirds. Increasing the yield rate is another component of active \nrecruitment strategies. \nWe have an extremely diverse pool of international applicants, which has resulted in one of the \nmost international graduate student populations in the country (on par with UBC). This is \nparticularly true at the doctoral level. At the same time, however, we have observed a decline in \nthe number of domestic doctoral applicants, contrary to the national trend. An effective domestic \ndoctoral recruitment strategy is one option for reversing this trend. \nBy contrast, international applicants remain a relatively small part of the applicant pool for our \ncourse-based Master’s programs – contrary to much of the commentary concerning the \nattractiveness of these programs to international students. Marketing and promotion of our \ncourse-based Master’s programs may be necessary as the University proceeds with the \ndevelopment of new programs. \nExpanding the pool of qualified First Nations, Métis, and Inuit (FNMI) applicants requires careful \nplanning and recruitment strategies.  \nThere is gender imbalance in the number of applications for different degrees, with doctoral \nprograms attracting almost twice as many male as female applicants, while the proportion is \nalmost reversed for course-based Master’s programs. Female applicants tend to have a higher \nyield rate than their male counterparts. \n3 Our institutional graduate admission requirements are having a degree equivalent to a U of A baccalaureate degree, having an \nadmission GPA of at least 3.0, and a reasonable English Language Proficiency score if from an institution where the main language \nof instruction was not English. Numerous programs set higher standards. At the same time, programs can waive some of the \nstandards for a particular applicant in the light of other circumstances.  \n14 \n2.1 Graduate Admissions \nThe following graph shows the total number4 of applications for admission to graduate \nprograms, the number of offers of admissions made, and the number of subsequent \nregistrations. (Please note some applicants may have submitted more than one application.)  \nSource: FGSR Statistics \nAnecdotal evidence suggests that the trend at University of Alberta – a peak in 2012-13 leading \nto a period of decline – is consistent with national trends, particularly at the PhD level. However, \nup-to-date U-15 data is not yet available to confirm this.  \n4 The numbers given are for those applying in a given academic year. The date of first registration is frequently in a different \nacademic year. Unlike the vast majority of undergraduate students, almost 15% of graduate students do not start in the fall term. \n5948 \n6469 \n7301 7157 \n8076 \n9184 9259 \n10309 9855 \n9298 \n2696 2834 \n3224 3180 3619 3383 3323 3464 3402 3040 \n1888 1945 2202 2226 2509 2393 2342 2558 2538 2063 \n0\n2000\n4000\n6000\n8000\n10000\n12000\nHe\nad\nco\nun\nt \nAcademic Year \nApplications Applicants Admitted Applicants Registered\n15 \n2.2 Domestic Graduate Admissions \nSource: FGSR Statistics \nBetween 2011-2012 and 2014-2015, the number of domestic doctoral applicants fell by more \nthan 30%, the number of Master’s thesis-based applicants was almost stable, and the number \nof Master’s course-based applicants grew by almost 24%. The declines in offers and \nregistrations were in both doctoral and Master’s course-based pools.  \nThe ten-year trend, however, shows an increase of 23% in applications and, concomitantly, a \ndecline from 42% (1397 of 3352) to 39% in our admit rate. A decline in the number of applicants \nadmitted demonstrates greater selectiveness: an important quality indicator. \nIt is noteworthy that yield rates for domestic applicants have always been significantly higher \nthan for international (around 75%, versus under 60%). \n3352 3521 \n3981 \n3732 3723 \n3955 3755 \n4456 4449 4132 \n1833 1903 \n2102 1945 2037 1980 1857 1923 1892 1594 \n1397 1435 1586 1495 1588 1551 1456 1560 1515 \n1165 \n0\n1000\n2000\n3000\n4000\n5000\n6000\nHe\nad\nco\nun\nt \nAcademic Year \nApplications Applicants Admitted Applicants Registered\n16 \n2.3 International Graduate Admissions  \nApplicants on a study permit form an increasingly large part of the total graduate applicant pool.  \nSource: FGSR Statistics \nAs is the case with domestic admissions, the ten-year trend here is remarkable, with \ninternational applicants more than doubling (at their peak) over the last decade. Admission rate \nin 2005-06 was 30% (734 of 2449); in 2014-15 it is down to 25.6%. Again, this is an indication of \nquality. \nThe decline of more than 11% since 2012-2013 is mainly in the doctoral (19%) and Master’s \nthesis-based (10%) pools. The number of offers and registrations has grown for the international \ndoctoral and Master’s course-based pools. The increase in doctoral offers on a shrinking pool of \napplicants is an issue to be addressed.  \n2449 \n2828 \n3166 3243 \n4177 \n5050 5312 \n5604 \n5106 4971 \n734 836 \n1000 1087 \n1431 1252 1317 1319 1227 1275 \n382 422 504 597 \n792 714 768 814 797 761 \n0\n1000\n2000\n3000\n4000\n5000\n6000\nHe\nad\nco\nun\nt \nAcademic Year \nApplications Applicants Admitted Applicants Registered\n17 \nAdmissions Grade Point Average (AGPA)  \nThe admissions grade point average (AGPA)5 is one of the basic eligibility criteria for graduate \nadmissions, although it is not usually a final determining factor. The remainder consists of those who have left the University without any \ncredential. \nAttrition rates and attrition times (how long it takes a graduate student to stop having a \nregistration without having obtained a credential) are matters that the University has to \nunderstand and monitor.   \nThe issue is subtle: The attrition rate in 2005-2006 was about 21.5%, while the attrition rate in \n2009-2010 was about 14.7%.
